25/11/2022, 15:30 - Aniket Babar: package Fcfs;
import java.util.*;

public class Fcfs1 {
	
	public static void main(String args[]) {
		Scanner sc = new Scanner(System.in);
		System.out.println("enter no. of process:");
		int n = sc.nextInt();
	
		
		int pid[] = new int[n];
		int at[] = new int[n];
		int bt[] = new int[n];
		int ct[] = new int[n];
		int tat[] = new int[n];
		int wt[] = new int[n];
		int f[] = new int[n];
		int tot=0,st=0;
		float avgwt=0,avgtat=0;
		
		for(int i=0;i<n;i++) {
			System.out.println("enter process " + (i+1) + " Arrival time");
			at[i]=sc.nextInt();
			System.out.println("enter process " + (i+1) + " burst time ");
			bt[i]=sc.nextInt();
			pid[i]=i+1;
			f[i]=0;
			
		boolean a=true;
		while(true) {
			int c=n,min=999;
			if(tot==n) { 
				break;}
			
		for(int j=0;j<n;j++) {
			if((at[j]<=st)  && (f[j]==0) && (bt[j]<min) )
			{
				min=bt[j];
				c=j;
				}
			
			}
			if(c==n) {
				st++;}
			else
			{
				
				ct[c]=st+bt[c];
				st+=bt[c];
				tat[c]=ct[c]-at[c];
				wt[c]=tat[c]-bt[c];
				f[c]=1;
				tot++;
				
					
				}
			}
		System.out.println("\n pid  arrival  burst   complete  turn  waiting ");
		
		for(int k=0;k<n;k++) {
			avgwt+= wt[k];
			avgtat+= tat[k];
			System.out.println(pid[k] + "\t" +at[k]+ "\t" + bt[k]+"\t"+ct[k]+"\t" +tat[k]+"\t"+wt[k]);
		}
		System.out.println("\n average tat is "+ (float)(avgtat/n));
		System.out.println("\n average wt is "+ (float)(avgwt/n));
		sc.close();
		
			
		
			
		}
		
	}
}

28/05/2023, 11:58 - Pushpak: import pyaes

# A 256 bit (32 byte) key
key = "This_key_for_demo_purposes_only!"
plaintext = "Implementing AES algorithm using python"

# key must be bytes, so we convert it
key = key.encode('utf-8')

aes = pyaes.AESModeOfOperationCTR(key)    
ciphertext = aes.encrypt(plaintext)

# show the encrypted data
print (f'cipher_text=\n{ciphertext}')

# DECRYPTION
# CRT mode decryption requires a new instance be created
aes = pyaes.AESModeOfOperationCTR(key)

# decrypted data is always binary, need to decode to plaintext
decrypted = aes.decrypt(ciphertext).decode('utf-8')

# True
print ('decrypted =='+ plaintext)

28/05/2023, 15:48 - Aniket Babar: from Cryptodome.Cipher import DES
from Cryptodome.Util.Padding import pad, unpad
from Cryptodome.Random import get_random_bytes

def encrypt(plaintext, key):
    cipher = DES.new(key, DES.MODE_ECB)
    ciphertext = cipher.encrypt(pad(plaintext, DES.block_size))
    return ciphertext

def decrypt(ciphertext, key):
    cipher = DES.new(key, DES.MODE_ECB)
    plaintext = unpad(cipher.decrypt(ciphertext), DES.block_size)
    return plaintext

# Example usage
key = get_random_bytes(8)  # 64-bit key
plaintext = b'This is a secret message.'

encrypted = encrypt(plaintext, key)
decrypted = decrypt(encrypted, key)

print("Plaintext:", plaintext)
print("Encrypted:", encrypted)
print("Decrypted:", decrypted)
28/05/2023, 15:49 - Aniket Babar: from Crypto.Cipher import AES
from Crypto.Util.Padding import pad, unpad
from Crypto.Random import get_random_bytes

def encrypt(plaintext, key):
    cipher = AES.new(key, AES.MODE_CBC)
    ciphertext = cipher.encrypt(pad(plaintext, AES.block_size))
    return cipher.iv + ciphertext

def decrypt(ciphertext, key):
    iv = ciphertext[:AES.block_size]
    ciphertext = ciphertext[AES.block_size:]
    cipher = AES.new(key, AES.MODE_CBC, iv)
    plaintext = unpad(cipher.decrypt(ciphertext), AES.block_size)
    return plaintext

# Example usage
key = get_random_bytes(16)  # 128-bit key
plaintext = b'This is a secret message.'

encrypted = encrypt(plaintext, key)
decrypted = decrypt(encrypted, key)

print("Plaintext:", plaintext)
print("Encrypted:", encrypted)
print("Decrypted:", decrypted)

        if char.isupper():

def caesar_decode(text, shift):
    result="" 
    for char in text:
        if char.isupper():
            result += chr((ord (char) + shift - 65) % 26 +65 )
        elif char.isupper():
            result += chr((ord (char) - shift-65) % 26+ 65)
        else:
            result += char
    return result

28/05/2023, 19:44 - Pushpak: def caesar_encrypt(plaintext, key):
    ciphertext = ""
    for char in plaintext:
        if char.isalpha():
            ascii_offset = ord('a') if char.islower() else ord('A')
            encrypted_char = chr((ord(char) - ascii_offset + key) % 26 + ascii_offset)
            ciphertext += encrypted_char
        else:
            ciphertext += char
    return ciphertext


def caesar_decrypt(ciphertext, key):
    plaintext = ""
    for char in ciphertext:
        if char.isalpha():
            ascii_offset = ord('a') if char.islower() else ord('A')
            decrypted_char = chr((ord(char) - ascii_offset - key) % 26 + ascii_offset)
            plaintext += decrypted_char
        else:
            plaintext += char
    return plaintext


# Example usage
plaintext = "Hello, World!"
key = 3

encrypted_text = caesar_encrypt(plaintext, key)
print("Encrypted text:", encrypted_text)

decrypted_text = caesar_decrypt(encrypted_text, key)
print("Decrypted text:", decrypted_text)

def encrypt(plaintext, key):
    ciphertext = ''
    for i in plaintext:
        if i.isalpha():
            ascii_offset = ord('a') if i.islower() else ord('A')
            encrypted_text = chr((ord(i)-ascii_offset+key) % 26+ascii_offset)

28/05/2023, 23:36 - Pushpak: from collections import deque

# BFS algorithm
def bfs(graph, start):
    visited = set()
    queue = deque([start])
    
    while queue:
        vertex = queue.popleft()
        if vertex not in visited:
            print(vertex, end=" ")
            visited.add(vertex)
            queue.extend(graph[vertex] - visited)

# DFS algorithm
def dfs(graph, start):
    visited = set()
    stack = [start]
    
    while stack:
        vertex = stack.pop()
        if vertex not in visited:
            print(vertex, end=" ")
            visited.add(vertex)
            stack.extend(graph[vertex] - visited)

# Example usage:

# Graph represented as an adjacency list
graph = {
    'A': {'B', 'C'},
    'B': {'A', 'D', 'E'},
    'C': {'A', 'F'},
    'D': {'B'},
    'E': {'B', 'F'},
    'F': {'C', 'E'}
}

start_vertex = 'A'

print("BFS traversal:")
bfs(graph, start_vertex)

print("\nDFS traversal:")
dfs(graph, start_vertex)
29/05/2023, 12:02 - Aniket Babar: The Advanced Encryption Standard (AES) is a widely used symmetric encryption algorithm. It operates on blocks of data and uses a secret key to encrypt and decrypt the information. Here is a step-by-step explanation of the AES algorithm:

1. Key Expansion:
   - The secret key is expanded to generate a set of round keys, which will be used in the encryption and decryption process. This involves applying a series of transformations to the key.

2. Initial Round:
   - The input data block is divided into a matrix of bytes (4x4).
   - Each byte is XORed with the corresponding byte from the round key.

3. Rounds:
   - AES consists of multiple rounds, each involving a specific set of operations.
   - Each round, except the last one, consists of the following steps:
     - Byte Substitution: Each byte in the matrix is substituted using a substitution box (S-box). The S-box replaces each byte with a corresponding value based on a predefined lookup table.
     - Row Shifting: The bytes in each row of the matrix are cyclically shifted to the left. The first row remains unchanged, the second row shifts by one position, the third row shifts by two positions, and the fourth row shifts by three positions.
     - Column Mixing: The columns of the matrix are mixed using a mathematical operation known as the MixColumns transformation. This step provides diffusion and enhances the security of the algorithm.
     - Round Key Addition: Each byte in the matrix is XORed with the corresponding byte from the round key.

4. Final Round:
   - The final round does not include the MixColumns transformation.
   - It consists of the following steps:
     - Byte Substitution.
     - Row Shifting.
     - Round Key Addition.

5. Output:
   - The resulting matrix is the encrypted/decrypted data block.
   - The bytes of the matrix are read column by column and concatenated to obtain the final output.

These steps describe the basic operation of the AES algorithm. The number of rounds performed depends on the key size: 10 rounds for a 128-bit key, 12 rounds for a 192-bit key, and 14 rounds for a 256-bit key.
29/05/2023, 12:02 - Aniket Babar: The Data Encryption Standard (DES) algorithm is a symmetric key encryption algorithm that operates on 64-bit blocks of data. Here are the step-by-step instructions for the DES algorithm:

Key Generation:

Select a 64-bit encryption key, which consists of 8 parity bits and 56 effective key bits. The parity bits are used for error detection and are not used in the encryption process.
Apply a key schedule algorithm to the 56 effective key bits to generate 16 round subkeys, each consisting of 48 bits.
Initial Permutation (IP):

The 64-bit plaintext block is subjected to an initial permutation, rearranging the bits according to a specific permutation table.
Splitting:

The permuted 64-bit block is divided into two halves, each containing 32 bits: the left half (L0) and the right half (R0).
Feistel Network (16 rounds):

Perform 16 rounds of encryption, using a common structure known as the Feistel network. In each round, the following steps are performed:
a. Expansion Permutation (E):

Expand the 32-bit right half (R) to 48 bits using an expansion permutation table.
b. Key Mixing:

XOR the expanded right half with the current round's subkey.
c. Substitution Boxes (S-boxes):

Divide the 48-bit result into eight 6-bit blocks.
Apply each 6-bit block to a specific S-box, which performs a nonlinear substitution, replacing the 6-bit block with a 4-bit output.
The S-boxes provide the main source of confusion in the encryption process.
d. Permutation (P):

Permute the 32-bit output of the S-boxes using a permutation table.
e. XOR and Swap:

XOR the permuted output with the left half (L) from the previous round.
Swap the positions of the left half (L) and the right half (R), preparing for the next round.
Final Permutation (FP):

After completing the 16 rounds, swap the positions of the left half (L16) and the right half (R16).
Perform a final permutation on the combined 64-bit block using a specific permutation table.
Output:

The final permuted block is the ciphertext, which is a 64-bit encrypted representation of the original plaintext.
Note: In each round of DES, the same set of operations is applied but with a different subkey derived from the original encryption key. The decryption process for DES involves using the subkeys in reverse order.

15/06/2023, 00:11 - Aniket Babar: In Ruby, scalar types refer to the basic data types that hold a single value. Here are the scalar types in Ruby and some of their operations:

Integer: Represents whole numbers (positive, negative, or zero).

Addition: +
Subtraction: -
Multiplication: *
Division: /
Modulo (remainder): %
Exponentiation: **
Float: Represents floating-point numbers (numbers with a decimal point).

Addition: +
Subtraction: -
Multiplication: *
Division: /
Exponentiation: **
Boolean: Represents true or false values.

Logical AND: &&
Logical OR: ||
Logical NOT: !
String: Represents a sequence of characters.

Concatenation: +
Length: .length or .size
Access individual characters: []
Substring extraction: .slice
String interpolation: #{} or %{}
Symbol: Represents immutable, interned names. Symbols start with a colon (:).

Used as identifiers in Ruby, often as keys in hashes.
Nil: Represents the absence of a value.

Typically used to indicate the absence of an object or the result of an operation that does not return a value.

26/07/2023, 12:28 - Aniket Babar: It is commonly known among Data Scientists that data cleaning and preprocessing make up a major part of a data science project. And, you will probably agree with me that it is not the most exciting part of the project. Wouldn't it be great if this part could be automated?

✅ AutoClean helps you exactly with that: it performs preprocessing and cleaning of data in Python in an automated manner, so that you can save time when working on your next project.

AutoClean supports:

👉 Handling of duplicates [ NEW with version v1.1.0 ]
👉 Various imputation methods for missing values
👉 Handling of outliers
👉 Encoding of categorical data (OneHot, Label)
👉 Extraction of datatime values

26/07/2023, 23:05 - Aniket Babar: Title: Automated Data Cleaning for Efficient Data Science Analysis

Abstract:

In the realm of data science, data cleaning is a fundamental yet labor-intensive process that significantly impacts the quality and reliability of analyses. This project aims to streamline and automate the data cleaning phase, providing data scientists with a robust and efficient solution to enhance data preparation and analysis workflows.

The project leverages a combination of programming languages, data manipulation libraries (such as Python's Pandas and R's dplyr), and regular expressions to develop a systematic approach to data cleaning. By implementing rule-based systems and machine learning algorithms, the automated solution can efficiently handle various data issues, including missing values, outliers, and inconsistent formats.

The workflow automation tools, like Apache Airflow or Luigi, are utilized to manage and schedule the data cleaning routines, allowing for easy integration into existing data pipelines. Additionally, data profiling tools are employed to gain insights into the dataset's quality and distribution, facilitating an informed and systematic cleaning process.

The project's architecture ensures scalability, enabling the automated data cleaning to efficiently handle large datasets, thus reducing processing time and resource consumption. Version control and containerization further contribute to the project's reproducibility and portability.

Overall, this project aims to provide data scientists with a comprehensive and reliable automated data cleaning solution, saving valuable time, reducing errors, improving data quality, and empowering better decision-making through clean and consistent datasets for data science analyses.
26/07/2023, 23:05 - Aniket Babar: The automation of data cleaning in a data science project can be built using a combination of various technologies and tools. The specific technologies used may vary depending on the project's requirements, but here is a general description of the technologies commonly involved in such projects:

1. **Programming Languages**: Data cleaning automation typically involves the use of programming languages to develop the necessary scripts and algorithms. Python and R are two of the most popular languages for data science and data manipulation tasks. Python's libraries like Pandas and NumPy and R's packages like dplyr are often used for data cleaning operations.

2. **Data Manipulation Libraries**: Libraries like Pandas (for Python) and dplyr (for R) provide powerful tools for data wrangling, cleaning, and transformation. These libraries offer a wide range of functions and methods to handle missing data, filter, reshape, merge, and aggregate datasets.

3. **Regular Expressions**: Regular expressions (regex) are useful for pattern matching and data extraction tasks. They can be employed to identify and clean data that follows specific patterns, such as dates, phone numbers, or email addresses.

4. **Data Visualization Libraries**: Data visualization tools like Matplotlib (Python) or ggplot2 (R) are beneficial during data cleaning to help understand data distributions, identify outliers, and visualize potential data issues.

5. **Machine Learning Algorithms**: In some cases, machine learning algorithms can be used for automated data imputation, outlier detection, and data classification. For example, decision trees or clustering algorithms can help identify and handle outliers or missing data.

6. **Rule-based Systems**: Automation of data cleaning often involves setting up rule-based systems where predefined rules are applied to identify and clean specific data issues. These rules can be implemented as logical conditions or transformation operations.

7. **Data Profiling Tools**: Data profiling tools can be used to automatically analyze datasets, identify data quality issues, and provide summary statistics. These insights help in understanding the data and planning the data cleaning process.

8. **Workflow Automation Tools**: Workflow automation tools like Apache Airflow or Luigi can help manage and schedule the data cleaning process, making it easier to run and maintain the cleaning routines on a regular basis.

9. **Version Control**: Version control systems like Git are essential for collaborative data science projects. They allow tracking changes, collaborating with team members, and reverting to previous versions if needed.

10. **Cloud Services**: Cloud platforms like AWS, Azure, or Google Cloud offer scalable infrastructure for data processing, storage, and analysis. They can be utilized for handling large datasets and distributed computing tasks efficiently.

11. **Containerization**: Containerization tools like Docker enable packaging data cleaning workflows and dependencies into containers, making the deployment and execution of the automation process more portable and reproducible.

The combination of these technologies enables data scientists and engineers to design, implement, and maintain automated data cleaning pipelines that streamline the data preparation process, improving the overall efficiency and accuracy of data analysis in data science projects.
26/07/2023, 23:05 - Aniket Babar: Automating data cleaning in a data science project offers several significant benefits. Data cleaning is a critical and often time-consuming step in the data analysis process, and automation can help streamline this task, leading to more efficient and accurate data analysis. Here are some key benefits of automating data cleaning:

1. **Time-saving**: Automation reduces the manual effort required for data cleaning, which can be particularly advantageous when dealing with large datasets. It accelerates the data preparation phase and allows data scientists to focus on higher-level tasks and more in-depth analysis.

2. **Consistency**: Automated data cleaning ensures that the same cleaning processes are applied consistently across the entire dataset. This consistency helps avoid errors that could arise from human oversight or inconsistency in manual cleaning.

3. **Error reduction**: Automated data cleaning routines are less prone to human errors compared to manual cleaning. Human mistakes, such as overlooking certain data issues or applying incorrect transformations, can be minimized with well-designed automated procedures.

4. **Scalability**: As the size of the dataset grows, manual data cleaning becomes increasingly impractical. Automation enables data scientists to handle large datasets efficiently, making the data cleaning process scalable.

5. **Reproducibility**: Automated data cleaning workflows are easily reproducible. Once a cleaning routine is defined and implemented, it can be applied consistently to new datasets or updated versions of existing data without the need to recreate the process from scratch.

6. **Flexibility**: Automation allows for the easy modification and adaptation of data cleaning steps. If new data issues are discovered or changes in data sources occur, adjustments can be made to the automated process quickly.

7. **Data quality improvement**: By implementing predefined data cleaning rules and procedures, automation can help improve data quality by standardizing, normalizing, and removing inconsistencies or inaccuracies from the data.

8. **Resource efficiency**: Automated data cleaning helps optimize resource allocation, as it requires fewer human resources to complete the cleaning phase, leaving data scientists and analysts with more time and resources for other tasks.

9. **Robustness**: Automation can be designed to handle a wide range of data issues and anomalies effectively, making the data cleaning process more robust and capable of handling diverse datasets.

10. **Better decision-making**: Clean, consistent, and high-quality data are essential for making accurate and informed decisions. By automating data cleaning, organizations can ensure that their analyses and decisions are based on reliable data.

Overall, automation of data cleaning in data science projects empowers data scientists and analysts to work more efficiently, derive better insights, and make data-driven decisions with confidence. It contributes to the overall success and effectiveness of data science initiatives.

29/10/2023, 17:34 - Aniket Babar: def total_energy(intial_energy, rate, time_n):
  """Calculates the total energy produced in a science research lab.

  Args:
    intial_energy: The initial energy produced upon combining the nuclear chemicals (X).
    rate: The consistent rate of change of energy.
    time_n: The Nth second for which the scientist wishes to calculate the total energy produced.

  Returns:
    The total energy produced by the Nth second.
  """

  total_energy = intial_energy + rate * time_n
  return total_energy


# Read the input values
intial_energy = int(input("Enter initial energy: "))
rate = int(input("Input rate: "))
time_n = int(input("Input time: "))

# Calculate the total energy produced by the Nth second
total_energy = total_energy(intial_energy, rate, time_n)

# Print the total energy
print("The total energy is: ", total_energy)
29/10/2023, 17:52 - Aniket Babar: def find_max_energy(energies):
  """Finds the sum of energy of the two chemicals which produces maximum energy on reaction.

  Args:
    energies: A list of integers representing the energies of the chemicals.

  Returns:
    An integer representing the sum of energy of the two chemicals which produces maximum energy on reaction.
  """

  max_energy = -float('inf')
  max_energy_sum = 0

  for i in range(len(energies)):
    for j in range(i + 1, len(energies)):
      energy = energies[i] * energies[j]
      if energy > max_energy:
        max_energy = energy
        max_energy_sum = energies[i] + energies[j]

  return max_energy_sum


def main():
  """Main function."""

  num_chemicals = int(input())
  energies = list(map(int, input().split()))

  max_energy_sum = find_max_energy(energies)

  print(max_energy_sum)


if __name__ == '__main__':
  main()

28/11/2023, 01:38 - Aniket Babar: Sort the jobs based on their profits in descending order:

This step ensures that you consider jobs with higher profits first.
Initialize an array to keep track of time slots and a variable to store the total profit:

The array will be used to mark time slots when jobs are scheduled.
Set the total profit initially to 0.
Iterate through the sorted jobs:

For each job in the sorted list:
Find the maximum available time slot for the job. This is usually the maximum deadline possible while ensuring the slot is not already occupied.
If a time slot is found, mark it as occupied and add the profit of the job to the total profit.
Continue the iteration until all jobs are considered or all time slots are filled.
28/11/2023, 02:01 - Aniket Babar: Sort the jobs in decreasing order of their profit.
Find the highest deadline among all deadlines and draw a Gantt chart up to that deadline.
Now, we need to assign time slots to individual job ids. Now pick each job one by one and check if the maximum possible time slot for the job, i.e., its deadline, is assigned to another job or not.
Otherwise, search for any empty time slot less than the deadline of the current job.
Continue this process until all the feasible jobs are allocated to their time slot. In the end, we can calculate the profit for all the allocated feasible jobs.
28/11/2023, 02:46 - Pushpak: Dynamic Programming:

Dynamic programming is an algorithmic optimization technique used to solve problems by breaking them down into simpler subproblems and solving those subproblems just once, storing their solutions to avoid redundant computations.
It is often used for optimization problems and involves solving larger problems by combining the solutions to their smaller subproblems.
Dynamic programming typically involves a bottom-up or top-down approach and requires the optimal substructure and overlapping subproblems properties.
Common examples of problems solved using dynamic programming include the knapsack problem, shortest path problems, matrix chain multiplication, etc.
Control Abstraction:

Control abstraction is a programming concept that involves hiding the implementation details of a specific task or functionality behind a well-defined interface or abstraction.
It allows programmers to create functions, procedures, or modules that encapsulate certain functionalities, allowing the user to interact with them using a high-level interface without needing to understand the underlying implementation details.
Control abstraction promotes modularity, code reusability, and simplification of complex systems by breaking them into smaller, manageable parts.
Functions, classes, modules, and libraries are examples of control abstractions in programming languages. For instance, when using a function, the user doesn't need to know how the function works internally; they only need to know its inputs, outputs, and purpose.

09/12/2023, 18:20 - Pushpak: Certainly! 

**Inter-query parallelism** refers to the execution of multiple queries simultaneously or in parallel. This means that different queries are being executed concurrently, often in a multi-user environment, to improve overall system efficiency.

On the other hand, **intra-query parallelism** involves breaking down a single query into smaller parts or tasks that can be executed concurrently across multiple processing units or threads within a single query execution. This approach speeds up the execution of a single complex query by dividing it into smaller units that can be processed simultaneously.

In summary, inter-query parallelism deals with concurrent execution of multiple queries, while intra-query parallelism focuses on parallelizing the execution of a single query to enhance performance.

18/03/2024, 23:57 - Pushpak: Project Title: Gender and Age Detection using Facial Recognition

Objective:
The objective of this project is to develop a system that can accurately predict the gender and age of a person based on their facial features. By utilizing machine learning and computer vision techniques, the system will analyze images or video frames to determine whether the individual is male or female, as well as estimate their age range.

Prerequisites:

Proficiency in programming languages such as Python.
Knowledge of machine learning concepts, particularly in image processing and classification.
Familiarity with libraries such as OpenCV, TensorFlow, or PyTorch for image processing and deep learning.
Access to a dataset containing labeled images of faces with corresponding gender and age information for training the model.
Little Theory:
Gender and age detection using facial recognition involves several key steps:

Data Collection: Gather a dataset of facial images labeled with the person's gender and age range.

Preprocessing: Preprocess the images to standardize them for input into the model. This may involve tasks such as resizing, normalization, and cropping.

Feature Extraction: Extract relevant features from the facial images that can be used for classification. This might include facial landmarks, texture patterns, or color information.

Model Training: Train a machine learning model using the labeled dataset. Common techniques include deep learning approaches such as convolutional neural networks (CNNs).

Model Evaluation: Evaluate the trained model using a separate validation dataset to assess its performance in predicting gender and age.

Deployment: Deploy the trained model into a real-world application where it can analyze new images or video frames and make predictions in real-time.

Conclusion:
Gender and age detection using facial recognition is a challenging yet fascinating application of machine learning and computer vision. By leveraging advances in deep learning and image processing techniques, accurate predictions can be made based on facial features alone. This project provides valuable insights into the capabilities of modern AI systems and their potential applications in various domains such as security, marketing, and personalized user experiences.

19/03/2024, 03:10 - Pushpak: Load the pre-trained deep learning model for brain tumor detection.
Pre-process the input MRI image.
Feed the pre-processed image to the deep learning model to obtain the predicted class label If a brain tumor is detected:
Load the segmented brain tumor image.
Initialize the marching cubes algorithm for 3D visualization.
Iterate over the segmented brain tumor image.
For each voxel, identify the marching cube configuration.
Generate a triangle mesh for the marching cube configuration.
Add the triangle mesh to the 3D model of the brain tumor.
Output the predicted class label along with the 3D model of the brain tumor if detected.
